==PAGE:3==
Summary
When structures are situated in highly seismic locations and they are subjected to
earthquake loads, they suffer a big deterioration leading them to act in the nonlin-
ear regime by losing strength and stiffness. Therefore, one of the main researches
in seismic engineering nowadays is to try to predict the effect of an earthquake in a
structure when it is acting in the nonlinear regime, to be able to quantify the damages
that are induced.
Furthermore, the modal parameters are considered the global indexes that represent
the changes in structures under dynamic loads and therefore, if they are studied, the
damages will also be quantified
Because of that, this investigation studies the changes in the modal parameters of
a structure subjected to earthquake excitations. The aim is to be able to develop a
method where the stiffness matrix is obtained for every time step so that the changes
in stiffness are studied. Using OpenSees, an FE software that simulates the earth-
quake load over time, the extraction of the stiffness matrix every time instant will
be possible, to be able to run an eigenvalue analysis afterward, and track the main
dynamic parameters as period evolution and mode shapes over time.
The present study is based on a previous work developed for SDOF systems, that
has now been extended to observe several MDOF systems models developed and an-
alyzed using OpenSees.
These models are reinforced concrete frames that have a
fiber-based section to be able to have more accurate results because the section is
discretized in smaller uniaxial material sections.
The two main models that are studied are a simple two-storey and two-base frame,
which was used at the beginning to implement the method in a simpler way, and a
seven-storey and nine-base frame that is part of a real hotel structure in the USA.
This second model is used to corroborate the results obtained and discussed with the
smaller frame because it has been verified by a study that compares the responses
from OpenSees and some accelerograms that were used to record the real response.
Furthermore, the study involves results for several load factors that are compared
to obtain robust conclusions.
==PAGE:4==
ii
Summary
Overall, the effect of the stiffness degradation over time is studied as well as its
effect on the period elongation of several modes and in the mode shapes changes
over time. The residual period and mode shapes are also studied since they are very
important parameters to be tracked because they represent the damage state after
the earthquake has acted on the structure.
In conclusion, the importance of this study is to ensure that the method of extracting
the stiffness matrix of a system over time works and that it can be implemented in
reality for also MDOF systems to quantify and predict damages on structures. More-
over, the conclusions of the evolution of the different dynamic parameters with the
use of real models are also included in this work.
==PAGE:5==
Preface
This Master thesis was prepared at the Department of Civil Engineering at the Tech-
nical University of Denmark in fulfillment of the requirements for acquiring a Masters
of Sciences in Civil Engineering.
My personal motivation to get involved in this research started in my first semester
at DTU after taking the Seismic Engineering Course. It was my first time taking a
course in this field and a new world of possibilities opened in front of me at that time.
Moreover, I felt that even though the knowledge I gained during that time was huge,
I thought that if I had had some prior knowledge in the subject I could have taken
much more advantage of it. Because of that, I decided to spend my last semester
investigating and learning more in deep about Seismic Engineering.
Kongens Lyngby, July 2, 2020
Irati Larrinaga Calderon (s182636)

would not understand the topic, always providing their positive attitude and their

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
The engineers’ job is not only based on designing new structures but also assessing in
the best possible manner the existing ones. Especially in areas where seismic events
are prone to happen, the structures may suffer big deterioration due to earthquake
excitations leading to nonlinear behavior of the structure by losing strength and stiff-
ness. Therefore, there is a need to assess the behavior of those structures and be able
to predict them by quantifying the effect of the earthquakes on them.
This MSc thesis aims to analyze the changes in the modal parameters of several
Multiple Degree of Freedom Systems (MDOF) under the effect of the strong ground
motions of several earthquakes. The modal properties are considered to be the global
indexes that represent the changes in the structures and therefore, if they can be
quantified, the damages on the structures will also be possible to be quantified.
To identify those global indexes, some numerical frames are going to be settled using,
as mentioned, an MDOF system. In this analysis, the elastic regime will be overcome,
and a non-linear analysis will be required observing the effect of several earthquakes’
strong ground motions on the structure. Therefore, several time history analyses will
run and the changes in stiffness and strength under the effect of each of them will be
analyzed to be able to obtain sufficient data to achieve strong conclusions. Due to the
complexity of the numerical analysis and the amount of data available, a Non-linear
Finite Element Model software called OpenSees will be used to be able to capture all
the already mentioned parameters.
The data will provide enough information not only to track the period elongation
and the stiffness and strength degradation of these structures, but also to observe the
changes in the natural mode shapes (for several modes).
1.1
Objectives
The main objective of the present project is to track the evolution of the modal
properties of a system throughout the excitation of an earthquake in the nonlinear
regime. To do so, the generalized stiffness matrix of all the system is extracted at
every time step using OpenSees being able to assess its stiffness degradation over time.
==PAGE:24==
2
1 Introduction
From the stiffness matrices, the period of the system can be obtained, and there-
fore it is possible to analyze its period elongation and the residual periods after the
shock. Running an eigenvalue analysis for each captured stiffness matrix, will make
possible to obtain the period every instant. By doing so the period elongation will
be studied.
Furthermore, those results will also allow the residual periods to be analyzed . The
residual period is a very important parameter to be observed since it provides infor-
mation about the structural damage after the earthquake.
As this study is focusing on MDOF systems, the mode shapes evolution will also
be evaluated. Running a modal analysis, the vibration shape of the system can be
determined giving additional information about the nonlinear behavior of the struc-
tures. The mode shapes are also obtained from the modal analysis. Thus, they are
directly dependent on the stiffness matrix and because of that, their variation is also
related to the degradation of the system. Besides, the eigenvalues, so the frequencies,
are correlated with the eigenvectors, mode shapes, and because of that it is expected
to be able to establish a relation between them.
The method that is used to obtain the stiffness matrix every time step is based on
a previous work [5] done for SDOF systems and adapted to be able to solve MDOF
systems.
1.2
Literature Review
The structural system’s period elongation is caused due to the damages in the struc-
ture, i.e. stiffness and strength degradation (hysteretic behavior). The loss of stiffness
indicated by this period change is believed to be primarily the result of cracking and
other types of degradation during the seismic excitation ([6] and [22]). The elongation
indicates the inelasticity of the system and damage accumulation.
There are several methods to determine period elongation on the literature due to
the seismic hazard. The most popular one is to compute the Fourier Spectra or any
other analysis in the frequency domain for the relative acceleration response to be
able to obtain the predominant inelastic period[22], [17], [16].
Even though the spectral acceleration at the fundamental period of a structure is
a widely used parameter for the assessment of the damage, the same structure can
yield different levels of inelastic deformation and therefore the intensity measure is
also needed to be introduced. The Spectral Ground motion intensity method was
used to obtain the period elongation [18].
Furthermore, literature also describes other ways of investigating and quantifying
period elongation. As an example, the displacement time histories of the roof of the
structure was used to determine the period elongation by finding the elongation of
==PAGE:25==
1.2 Literature Review
3
the first mode period only considering the free vibration after the earthquake effect
[19].
Another example developed establishes a relationship between damage indexes (i.e.
Park-Ang) and the period elongation using IDARC computational tool reflecting the
changes in the stiffness matrix by the representation of the period elongation [1].
The hysteretic loops represent the amount of energy dissipated in the system. For
that, the system needs to be ductile so that it releases the maximum energy possible
from the response, being its behavior similar to the damping (hysteretic damping).
Therefore, running a hysteretic loop analysis the damages of a system can be detected,
localized and quantified. In other words, the nonlinear behavior of the system is rep-
resented in the loops showing stiffness degradation of each element [13].
Eurocode 8 [15] provides a range of periods where period elongation is observable
between 0.2Tels to 2Tels, whereas the American codes [12] lowers this range from
0.2Tels to 1.5Tels; Where, Tel is the fundamental elastic period.
Specifically, this
interval is suggested to respect the spectra-compatibility criteria of accelerograms to
adopt in linear/nonlinear time history analysis. The upper bound of the range is
determined based on the elongated fundamental period. It also aims to account for
the uncertainty in predicting Tel [22]. Moreover, [14] investigated and quantified the
correlation between the predominant period elongation of buildings designed accord-
ing to the modern seismic codes and various strong-motions and demand parameters.
The transformation of the MDOF system into SDOF system was required using the
strength and stiffness degradation and pinching and the principal conclusions for the
period elongation was that the first mode period elongation is correlated with the
frequency content of the seismic excitation. Systems with elastic period lower than
predominant period of excitation (0.5 < ≤Tel/Tm<1.0) leaded to Tin/Tel ratio be-
ing almost double than that observed in systems with Tin/Tel > 1.25. Additionally,
it was concluded that most degrading systems showed higher displacement ductility
demand (µd).
Period elongation results were described by Saeed Amiri Luigi Di Sarno [22] and
Katsanos and Sextos[17]. It was concluded that higher period elongation (Tin/Tel>2)
is expected in lower-to-moderate period structures (Tel ≤1.0s), whereas the more flex-
ible structures (Tel ̸=1.0s) suffer smaller elongation. The stiffer the structure is the
bigger the period elongation will be.
Furthermore, it was concluded that the stiffer structural systems (Tel ≤0.6s) with
high level of ductility (µ=0.6) experience higher lengthening (48 % on average) com-
pared to their equivalents with low level of ductility (µ=0.2).
The degrading stiffness ratio (αc) plays a minor role on the period elongation of the
system, hence, its effect is disregarded.
Finally, it was concluded that the strength pinching effect (ρy) affects the period elon-
gation more than the displacement pinching(ρx). Besides, the increase of strength
pinching effect leads to an increase in the period shift ratio, the contrary happens
==PAGE:26==
4
1 Introduction
with ρx though.
The use of Opensees for the calculation of the strength and stiffness degradation
has been previously analyzed and collected in the literature. First, it was showed
that the degradation and stiffness of box piers in the process of bidirectional cyclic
loading could be reflected by OpenSEES [11]. The study concluded demonstrating
that creating a fiber-based model out of the structural elements of reinforced concrete
the hysteretic and skeleton curves could be represented showing the results for both
stiffness and strength deterioration. Furthermore, the reinforced concrete section was
defined with concrete02 that considers the tension of concrete and steel02 materials.
On the other hand, it was created a new intensity measure that takes into account
the ground motion based on period elongation and the yield spectral acceleration of
the structure [18]. The correlation of this intensity measure with the calculated de-
mand parameter, in this case, inter-story drift, is investigated for 100 ground motion
records. 7 reinforced concrete frames were used for this analysis which was selected
so that there is a uniform distribution of fundamental period between 0.3s to 1.2s.
Those frames were analyzed in OpenSEES by creating a massless element frame,
with lumped mass at the mass center of each story and a fiber-based section with
distributed plasticity. The materials that were used for modeling the RC section were
concrete01, the modified Kent and Park concrete model, and steel01. Furthermore,
the damping ratio was considered to be 5% to be used with a stiffness proportional
Rayleigh damping.
Literature also covers the experimental results for the period elongation. Masi and
Vona[24] examined the results form experimental in situ and laboratory tests of the
period elongation (stiffness degradation) of a RC structure during and after strong
ground shaking. This comparison concludes that the period results varied very little
in the presence of very strong damages. Furthermore, the just mentioned study also
compares the formula provided by EC8 [15] with numerical and experimental expres-
sions leading to large deviations between them finishing the research suggesting a
revision of the empirical formulas of the code. The overall conclusion of the study
was that experimental tests on instrumented buildings subjected to moderate seismic
events showed low variation (15-20 %) of period values also in presence of heavy dam-
ages. However, higher variations in the period values were found in laboratory tests
on a scaled model subjected to strong ground motion (40-50 %).
Other papers also compare the measured and experimental tests of RC structures
where the period elongation is visible, i.e. [16]. This paper aimed to demonstrate
the state-of-knowledge on the response of RC buildings during strong ground motion
shaking towards period elongation. The study indicated that period elongation can
lead to periods of vibration up to 1.8 to 2.5 times the initial period, corresponding to
a stiffness degradation between 70-85 %
There are several other ways of quantifying the damages on the structure or detecting
them, i.e. observing the frequency changes of the structures. Pinho, Crowley, and
==PAGE:27==
1.2 Literature Review
5
Calvi [30] explained theoretically the results of monitored structures to find out the
changes in natural frequencies that are considered sensitive indicators of structural
integrity. Those changes were used with several methods to find structural damages.
The most important one is the use of the modal frequencies to locate the damages
but there is also sensitivity analysis, resonance frequency observation, etc.
Even though there is not much, it exists literature about the changes in modal pa-
rameters due to the loss of stiffness and strength on structures. Marcin Kowalsky
and Stanislav Pospil[33], for example, performed another experimental research, but
this time the aim was to observe the changes in the modal parameters due to the
development of cracks in the concrete. The tests that were developed were shaking
table experiments aiming full-scale structural damage identification and modal analy-
sis of reinforced concrete frames under progressive damage. Finally, it was found out
that there was a reduction of stiffness that was analyzed with the development of the
cracks and that the natural frequencies also presented falls. However, the fall was not
uniform being the fastest the first mode and slower 2nd and 3rd mode respectively.
The loss of the frequencies reached the 10% and the stiffness the 15%.
Mode shapes represent the deformation of a structure when subjected to earthquake
shakes. They are directly related to the stiffness and therefore the stiffness degrada-
tion causes changes in the mode shapes. Basu and Chakraborty[4] described Wavelet
analysis method to identify the time-varying stiffness and related it to the mode
shapes evolution for an MDOF system. It tracks the changes of the mode shapes con-
cerning a previous one so that the ratio between them is plotted to make conclusions
about the accuracy of the method and the differences between the modes.
There are several types of ways to track the hysteretic degradation of a system. The
most common model is the Ibarra-Medina-Krawinkler model [23], which captures the
strength and stiffness degradation caused by the structural damages. It is defined
by a backbone curve and some degradation parameters and there are three different
versions of it: the bi-linear model, the peak oriented model and the pinching model
that are defined in [23]. Some other researchers used this model for the estimation of
the deterioration, i.e. [28] and [20]. Furthermore, Medina, Ibarra and Krawinkler[23]
and Lignos and Krawinkler[21] extended its use in the OpenSees software.
There are some other models like Bouc Wen hysteretic model that are also useful
to analyze the dynamic responses of a time-varying stiffness structure. [4] analyses
the use of the latest version of this smooth hysteretic model (BWBN) and concludes
finding reasonable results that track the restoring forces in time.
On the other hand, the fiber-based model gives also the possibility of obtaining hys-
teretic responses and consider the degradation of stiffness and strength as some liter-
ature has described, being also this one a smooth model.( [11] and [18])

CHAPTER 2
Theoretical
Background
The present study focuses on the general structural dynamic theory due to the ex-
citation of an earthquake load. Furthermore, the research will focus on the analysis
of the nonlinear performance forcing the system to overcome the elastic regime, an
analyzing its performance under the effect of the dynamic load.
In the present section, the nonlinear equations are developed based on the simple
linear dynamic equilibrium in the case of undamaged structures, being the main goal
to analyzed MDOF systems. However, considering the MDOF system a set of SDOFs
the theory of the just mentioned one is needed to be explained first.
2.1
Equation of motion
2.1.1
Single Degree of Freedom System
Figure 2.1: Single degree of freedom system’s (SDOF) dynamic equilibrium.
Following a simple SDOF system example (Figure 2.1), the dynamic equilibrium is
represented as the sum of the inertia, damping, and stiffness forces being equal to the
external force (Newton’s second law).
m¨u + c ˙u + ku = p(t)
(2.1)
==PAGE:30==
8
2 Theoretical Background
However, the case of study this time includes cracking, yielding, and other types of
damages in the structure due to the effect of an earthquake. Therefore, the structure’s
deformation won’t be linearly elastic and the resisting force will be an implicit function
of deformation. Thus, the equation of motion will derive into:
m¨u + c ˙u + fS(u) = −m¨ug(t)
(2.2)
Where fS(u) represents the inelastic system’s force-deformation relation and m¨ug(t)
is the earthquake force on the structure. It acts on the mass and has a negative sign
because it goes in the opposite direction of the ground motion.
To solve the second-order differential equation 2.2 numerical methods are going to be
used.
2.1.2
Multiple Degree of Freedom System
Figure 2.2: Multiple degrees of freedom system’s (SDOF) dynamic equilibrium. (a)
MDOF system; (b) Free body diagram [10].
Even though for theoretical explanations of a system with an SDOF is acceptable,
in reality, the dynamic behavior of the structures involves simultaneous motions of
several masses in shapes that before the analysis are not known. Because of that, the
previously developed theory is extended to an MDOF system.
In this case, the external forces (p(t)) are also visualized as distributed among the
three components of the structure: the stiffness component or elastic forces (fs), the
mass component or inertia forces (fs) and the damping component or damping force
(fD).
p(t) = fs + fI + fD
(2.3)
==PAGE:31==
2.2 Numerical Methods.
9
This equation can be rewritten using the relation of the components with acceleration,
velocity, and displacement as developed for the SDOF system. However, when talking
about MDOF systems, those components will be matrices (Equation 2.4) instead of
single values, whose dimensions will depend on the degrees of freedom.
M ¨u + C ˙u + Ku = p(t)
(2.4)
Where K, M, and C are the stiffness, mass, and damping matrices respectively.
Figure 2.2 shows a 2 degree of freedom system. Following the simple dynamic equilib-
rium equation for each of the degrees of freedom the following equation is obtained:
[ m1
0
0
m2

¨u2
}

{ fD1

{ fs1
fs2
}

{ p1(t)
p2(t)
}
(2.5)
As observed, the dimensions of the matrices will depend on the number of degrees of
freedom of the system.
When talking about damaged systems, as explained in previous sections, the force
deformation relation is nonlinear and therefore, the resisting force vector fS and dis-
placement vector u are dependent on each other, therefore, fS needs to be expressed
as an implicit function of u:
fs = fs(u)
(2.6)
Furthermore, since the system in subjected to a ground motion acceleration, the
external force will have an inertia term, leading to the following a nonlinear dynamic
equation:
M ¨u + C ˙u + fs(u) = M ¨ug
(2.7)
Likewise SDOF, MDOF systems also require numerical methods to solve the differ-
ential equation shown above.
2.2
Numerical Methods.
The most popular implicit time integration method is the Newmark scheme. When
talking about non-linear systems, iterations on displacements, velocities and accel-
erations are required to remove errors introduced due to non-linearities.
In 1959,
Newmark developed a family of time-stepping methods based on those equations:

and define the variation of the acceleration each time step [7]. The most common
values for those two constants in order to obtain satisfactory results are γ = 1

2.2.1
Numerical Stability
The accuracy and stability of Newmark’s method lays on the definition of γ and
β parameters as well as on the size of the time step (∆t). The two most common
methods used are the Average Acceleration method (γ = 1
2, β = 1
4) and the Linear
==PAGE:33==
2.2 Numerical Methods.
11
Acceleration method (γ = 1
2, β = 1
6) .
By definition, a system is considered to be unconditionally stable when procedures
lead to a bounded solution regardless the time-step length, and Conditionally Sta-
ble when those bounded solutions are reached if the time step is shorter that some
stability limit [7]. In Newmark’s method a system is stable if:
∆t
Tn

(2.19)
Therefore, looking at the two methods mentioned above, The Average Acceleration
Method is unconditionally stable because the limit is in the infinite according to
Equation 2.19. On the contrary, Linear Acceleration Method is conditionally stable
bellow the limit of 0.551 (right side of Equation 2.19).
Moreover, when choosing the method another factor that affects the accuracy is
the computational error. All the integration methods give a numerical error when
computing them, however, following Chopra’s [8] comparison between the different
methods, it can be concluded that some are bigger than the others.

theoretical solution showing the computational error in the different
methods [8].
Figure 2.3 shows that the Central Difference Method is the least accurate in terms of
period and that bellow the stability limit the Linear Acceleration Method gives the
least period elongation. All of this is due to a computational error.

2 Theoretical Background
2.2.2
Non-linear solving Algorithms
Until now, the Newmark Integration Methods has been explained for restricting forces
that are in linear function of the deformation, and therefore, iteration was not required.
However, in the non-linear case, to solve those equations some algorithms are used
to solve the time series: Newton-Raphson, Modified Newton-Raphson, and Krylow-
Newton algorithm.
2.3
Modal Analysis.
Modal analysis is a process from which the dynamic properties of a system can be
obtained in terms of natural frequencies, damping factors, and mode shapes.
Using the free undamped (C=0) vibration equation of motion (2.20) and after de-
riving it, if the harmonic oscillation solution is used (2.21), the angular frequency
term appears in the equation.
M ¨u + Ku = 0
(2.20)
u(t) = veiωt
(2.21)
The generalized eigenvalue analysis is solved (2.22) to be able to obtain values for the
frequencies (eigenvalues) and the mode shapes (eigenvectors).
v(K −ω2M) = 0
(2.22)
Being v the mode shape vector and ω the natural angular frequency.
2.4
Rayleigh Damping.
The undamped modes determined by the general eigenvalue problem or modal anal-
ysis lead to uncoupling of the equations of motion if the damping matrix leads to
uncoupled modal damping. Rayleigh damping is the simplest example of this where
the damping matrix C is first mass-proportional and stiffness-proportional:
C = a0M
and
C = a1K
(2.23)
Where a0 and a1 are constants with units of sec−1 [9]. Furthermore, the generalized
damping for the nth mode respecting both proportionality is:
Cn = a1ω2
nMn
and
Cn = a1Kn
(2.24)
The modal damping ratio for a mass-proportional system is related to the a0 coeffi-
cient
ζn = a0

ωn
(2.25)

C = a0M + a1K
(2.27)

CHAPTER 3
Methodology
The present study’s objective is based on being able to computationally extract the
nonlinear stiffness matrix of an MDOF system during the excitation of an earthquake
at every instant (time-step). To do so, a robust theoretical knowledge has been gained
to establish a method in which, with the help of OpenSees, an FE model software,
users will be capable to track the dynamic properties of the defined damaged system
over-time.
Appendix A describes the functionality of ’printA’ command in OpenSees when ob-
taining the general system’s stiffness matrix straightforward for a system set up as
’fullGeneral’. The ’printA’ command output varies depending on the type of analysis.
If the analysis is static, it will directly return the stiffness matrix because the mass
and the damping are not taken into account in the equations.
However, if the analysis is a transient analysis, the printed stiffness matrix will be
the generalized matrix for the entire system which contains both, damping and mass
matrices.
Additionally, the Newmark integration method is set up to solve the algorithm in
the non-linear regime.
The method consists of 3 steps:
1. Elastic-pushover analysis or pseudo-static analysis, to obtain the initial stiffness
matrix.
2. Elastic transient analysis by assuming that there is no damping so that the
Mass matrix can be obtained from the OpenSEES output (Generalized stiffness
matrix of the entire system).
3. Non-linear transient analysis or dynamic analysis from which it is possible to
obtain the non-linear stiffness matrix at every time step and subsequently the
Damping matrix due to their mutual dependence.
In this section, the development of the method is described.
==PAGE:38==
16
3 Methodology
3.1
Elastic Pushover Analysis. Step 1
At this step, a simple static analysis is run, which means that no acceleration either
velocity is acting on the system so that the system’s initial stiffness or elastic stiffness
can be obtained from OpenSees.
Furthermore, a good way for the verification of
this step is to calculate by hand the generalized stiffness matrix or the stiffness of
the entire system because it would be equal to K0 since there is no influence from
damping or mass matrices. Thus, ’printA’ command will return A = K0
In this case, the output will be independent of time, meaning that during the entire
duration of the earthquake shake the initial or elastic stiffness will remain constant.
3.2
Elastic Transient Analysis. Step 2
A dynamic transient analysis with no damping (C = 0) is run at this stage and
therefore, the equation of motion is simplified to:
A = K0 + c1M
(3.1)
Where A is the generalized stiffness matrix of the whole system, K0 is the initial or
the elastic stiffness matrix, M is the mass matrix of the system and c1 is a constant
factor for the mass matrix composed of Newmark constants (β and γ) and the defined
time step for the analysis (∆t) expressed in Equation 2.17.
The key point, in this case, is that the mass (M) remains constant over time and
so it is the elastic stiffness matrix (K0).
Besides, K0 is obtained in the previous
step and since the full general stiffness matrix of the system (A0) is the output of
OpenSees, the mass matrix can be easily obtained:
M = (A −K0)
c1
(3.2)
As previously stated, using ’printA’ command in the transient analysis the full general
stiffness matrix of the system can be obtained containing in this case the mass matrix
also, because there is no damping in this stage.
Furthermore, A in this case is
independent of time as well, and therefore a single output is needed.
3.3
Nonlinear Transient Analysis. Step 3
Step 3 consists of a nonlinear dynamic transient analysis with damping stiffness matrix
(C ̸= 0). In this case, the dynamic equilibrium equation will be the following:
A(t) = K(t) + c0C(t) + c1M
(3.3)
Where,
==PAGE:39==
3.3 Nonlinear Transient Analysis. Step 3
17
• A(t) is the time-dependent full general system stiffness matrix
• M is the mass matrix of the system
• c0 and c1 are mass and damping proportional constant factors that depend on
Newmark constants (β and γ) and the defined time step of the analysis (∆t)
(see Equation 2.17)
• K(t) Is the time-dependent stiffness matrix
• C(t) Is the time-dependent damping matrix
Running a transient analysis with C ̸= 0 and using the ’printA’ command the gen-
eralized stiffness matrix of the entire system at every instant can be obtained form
OpenSees using a while-loop for all time steps. Since the matrix won’t be the same
at every time there will be the same number of A(t) matrices as defined time steps,
being able to obtain the results for the entire duration of the earthquake.
However, Equation 3.3 contains 2 unknown variables, K(t) and C(t), for a single
equation, and because of that, Rayleigh damping formulation will be needed.
According to the theory developed in section 2.4, Rayleigh damping provides pro-
portionality of the damping with the stiffness and mass matrices in the following
way:
C(t) = a0M + a1K(t)
(3.4)
This provides dependence of both unknown variables and therefore if that expres-
sion is substituted in Equation 3.3 the non-linear stiffness matrix can be obtained
(Equation 3.6) and consequently also the damping matrix (Equation 3.4).
A(t) = K(t) + c0(a0M + a1K(t)) + c1M
(3.5)
K(t) = A(t) −M(c1 + c0a0)
(1 + c0a1)
(3.6)
==PAGE:40==
18
==PAGE:41==
CHAPTER 4
Validation of the
Method
The process of obtaining the stiffness matrix of the system every time step is vali-
dated in the present chapter focusing on MDOF systems. As previously mentioned,
the MDOF system analysis has been based in a previously developed study for SDOF
systems [5], and therefore, the method for those systems has been already validated.
This chapter studies two different models to validate the method. The first one has
a simpler configuration, whereas the second model is more developed and complex.
The two models are the following:
• A one-storey and two-base frame with hollowed steel 02 section.
• A two-storey and two-base frame with fiber-based RC section composed of con-
crete 02 and steel 02 materials.
Furthermore, the ground motion that has been used for this purpose is El Centro
gorund motion, whose accelerogram is shown in Figure 4.1

4.1
One-storey steel frame
The one storey two base frame from Figure 4.2 is a 2D frame that is form with two 8m
columns (Lc) and one 8m beam (Lb). These elements are defined massless because
the mass is added in the nodes as 1tn lumped mass in the three directions, horizontal-
translational, vertical-translational, and rotational. It has 3 degrees of freedom in
each node and the bottom nodes are fixed in the three directions, resulting on a 6
DOFs system.
Figure 4.2: One-storey steel frame with lumped masses. a) Section b) overall geom-
etry and distribution of the degrees of freedom.
The model is fiber-based model meaning that the section is discretized in smaller uni-
axial material sections. The uniaxial material that has been defined in this specific
example is Giuffré-Menegotto-Pinto (’steel02’) with a yield strength (fy) of 355MPa
and an initial elastic tangent module (E0) of 20GPa. The details about this specific
materials and the modelling of the frame in OpenSees are further developed in Ap-
pendix A.
Moreover, all the three elements have a hollow square section with a side length(a)
of 200mm and a thickness(t) of 10mm and the damping ratio assigned to this model
is 0.02.
All the data about geometric and material parameters are summarized in Table 4.1.

4.1.2
Validation of the elastic transient analysis
The elastic transient analysis’ aim is to obtain the mass matrix. Since the beginning,
this methodology has assumed a constant mass over time obtained from the general-
ized stiffness matrix of an elastic system subjected to an earthquake excitation (using
also ’printA’ command) and a constant that is calculated from the Newmark integra-
tion parameters (γ and β) and the time step (∆t). (see section 3.2)
In conclusion, the mass is expected to be constant over time (Figure 4.3). Further-
more, a linearly elastic behaviour is expected because the materials at this step are
defined as elastic, therefore, a good way of verifying this step is to make sure that no
hysteretic loops are represented in the moment-curvature relation (Figure 4.4).

4.1.3
Validation of the non-linear dynamic analysis
The dynamic nonlinear analysis’ objective is to obtain the stiffness matrix over time by
running a time-history analysis. In this case, there are several ways of validating that
the model is correct and that the methodology is working. First of all it is expected to
see some degradation of the stiffness over time, which means that because the system
is not elastic anymore the stiffness won’t remain constant with time, as in the previous
step. Furthermore, the nonlinear behaviour is reflected also in the moment-curvature
relation which will stop being linear and it will show the hysteretic behaviour by the
representation of some loops.

Figure 4.5: Steel frame’s nonlinear behaviour representation of the system every
0.005sec. (a)the displacement of the top right node of the structure
(b)the stiffness degradation of the right column, (c) the moment curva-
ture relation of the bottom right column and (d)El-centro strong ground
motion accelerogram.
Figure 4.12 contains information about the top node displacement of the system, the
stiffness degradation occurring over the duration of the ground motion at an specific
section of the right side column, the nonlinear moment-curvature representation of the
hysteric behaviour that was just mentioned and the ground motion itself (El-centro
in this case). All of this is done simultaneously and it is represented for every 0.005sec.
All the results, moreover, are affected by a chosen load factor. The load factor is
a parameter that is used to scale the earthquake which in this research is important
because by increasing it, the system is forced to act in the nonlinear regime, which is
the case of study.
In addition, the hysteretic loop represents 4 different stages: i) reloading starting from
the origin in the outer side direction with a positive force, ii) unloading towards the
origin with positive force as well, iii)reloading in the outer direction with a negative
force, and iv)unloading towards the origin with a negative force. The development of
the loop also represents the transition from the elastic regime towards yielding, being
==PAGE:47==
4.2 Two-storey Reinforced Concrete frame
25
the first time instants linear, and then starting to develop the loops.
Furthermore, the stiffness remains constant until around 5 seconds where it starts
to degrade. This means that the system is in the linear regime until this moment.
Because of the properties of the materials, the stiffness degrades at some points during
the ground motion but, it always recovers the initial stiffness, meaning that there is
no residual stiffness reduction, hence the tangent stiffness is equal to the linear elastic
one due to the changes in direction. The response, therefore, looks as expected.
4.2
Two-storey Reinforced Concrete frame
Figure 4.6: 2D and two-storey frame with nodal lumped masses. (a) Overall geom-
etry. (B) RC Section of the elements and rebar distribution.
Since this study focuses on reinforced concrete sections it is important to verify also
the method for this kind of material properties. Because of that, a more complex
system is proposed in this section with a more complicated section to make sure that
the method is valid.
The model is a 2D frame composed of two-storeys and 2 bases. The mass is rep-
resented as 1tn lumped mass in each node and the bases are also fixed in the 3
directions as represented in Figure 4.6.
In this case, the system will have 12 DOFs, 3 in each node, with a beam span of
==PAGE:48==
26
4 Validation of the Method
8m (B) and a storey height of 8m (H1 and H2) as well. It is a reinforced concrete
section which is defined in OpenSees as a fiber-based section composed of two uniaxial
material combination. The advantage of using fiber-based models is that when the
section is in bending, some fibers will be in tension and others in compression and
this needs to be defined with the use of fibers.
In addition, the uniaxial materials in this case combine are a linear tension soft-
ening material (’concrete02’) for the section and ’steel02’ for the rebars. The reason
for choosing those materials is because they can capture the degradation which is the
main objective of the present study. All the details regarding the modeling and the
material properties are described in Appendix A.
All the elements have the same dimensions, a squared concrete section with a side
length of 250mm and two lines of 4 rebars of 12mm of diameter, indeed 4 bars on
the top layer and 4 on the bottom layer. Furthermore, the cover layer that has been
defined is 50mm thick and the damping ratio is 0.02. Thus, a common value has been
chosen.
Regarding the material properties, a steel strength of 500MPa has been assigned and
an elastic modulus of 210GPa. On the other side, the concrete strength is 30MPa
and its Young’s modulus 20GPa. Furthermore, the model is composed of 25 fibers.
All of the geometric and material parameters are summarized in Table 5.1 and 5.2

4.2.1
Validation of the initial pushover elastic analysis
The validation of the process to obtain the initial stiffness matrix is the same as with
the previous model. However, the analytical calculation of the initial stiffness matrix
is not as straightforward due to the mixed materials. Because of that this section
also includes some equation for the calculation for the total area and inertia for the
reinforced concrete section. The area is calculated as it follows:
At = Ac + (n −1) ∗As,top + (n −1) ∗As,bottom
(4.3)
Where Ac is the concrete section area, As is the steel area that might differ depending
on the layer, and n is the ratio between concrete and steel elasticity modulus.
n = Es/Ec
(4.4)
To calculate the inertia the gravity centre needs to be calculated. Even though this
specific model’s geometry provides the advantage of knowing without any further
calculation where it is located, the general equation for its calculation is demonstrated
bellow:
cg = Ac h
2 + (n −1)As,topdtop + (n −1)As,bottomdbottom
At
(4.5)
Where Ac and As are the concrete and steel section areas, n is the just mentioned
coefficient, h is the section height and dtop and dbottom are the distances from the top
part of the section to the gravity center of both rebar lines. Finally, the total inertia
of the element is going to be obtained:
It = bh3
12 +bh(gc−h
2 )2+(n−1)As,top(dtop−gc)2+(n−1)As,bottom(dbottom−cg)2 (4.6)

earthquake.
Moreover, looking at the stiffness degradation it is observable that the stiffness re-
mains constant or in the linear regime until around 5 seconds where it starts to
degrade. The decay is considerable and that is reflected in the moment-curvature
loop where at the first instants remains linear but when the stiffness starts to de-
grade the loops start to grow.
As mentioned, there is a difference in the stiffness degradation between this model
and the previous one. The initial stiffness is not recovered over time as it was before,
and that means that there is a residual stiffness reduction on the system. This is due
to the material law, that in this case is provided by the concrete material.
==PAGE:53==
4.3 Validation of the fundamental elastic period
31
4.3
Validation of the fundamental elastic period
Besides, there is another verification that must be done in the third step. The defined
materials are different in OpenSees in steps 2 and 3 because to obtain the mass ma-
trix, the generalized stiffness matrix obtained at the second step has to be constant
over time and therefore, the materials defined in the elastic transient analysis, are
indeed elastic. However, in the third step or nonlinear analysis, the materials need to
be defined nonlinear to be able to track the degradation. Because of that, the models
are slightly different due to this change in the materials and therefore an additional
check needs to be done. The fundamental elastic period needs to be the same in both
steps because otherwise, the structure will be different. Running a simple eigenvalue
analysis in OpenSees for both models (with the changes from elastic to nonlinear ma-
terial definition) will need to provide the fundamental period.
For more information regarding the definition of the elastic and nonlinear elements
Appendix A can be checked.
As a final validation check, moreover, it is important that after running the method-
ology and obtaining all the stiffness matrices over time, the period or the first mode
at t=0sec is the same as the previously calculated eigenvalue analysis periods in both
transient and nonlinear models so that it is confirmed that their natural frequency is
the same.
Taking as an example the first model described, the initial periods’ values are go-
ing to be shown to demonstrate the validation of the method in terms of elastic
fundamental period.
Table 4.6: Comparison of the elastic fundamental period of the first mode at three

Because of that, in this section, the period evolution has been verified by making the

matrix in the linear or elastic regime shouldn’t suffer any change even after the time
history analysis and like that, it is possible to assure the way of obtaining the stiffness
matrix of the system every time step. The period is obtained from every time step’s
stiffness matrix.

==PAGE:57==
4.5 Validation of the residual period
35
4.5
Validation of the residual period
By definition, the residual period is the period of the structure after the earthquake
force has been applied. It is directly connected with the residual stiffness, and due
to the material properties a constant residual deteriorated stiffness is expected from
this model.
To obtain realistic results, 25 seconds of 0m/s2 acceleration has been added to El-
Centro ground motion to make the system stabilize and avoid a sudden stop of the
response. The reality is that right after the earthquake stops shaking, the system is
not stable, and therefore, by adding those points the constant stiffness will be induced.
The response, the stiffness evolution, and the hysteresis have been plot every time
instant at the same time to see the behavior. As it was expected and has already
been discussed over this report at the very beginning of the ground motion the stiff-
ness remains constant behaving in the linear regime. However, after 6.5 seconds the
stiffness degrades entering the non-linear regime. Finally, when the acceleration is
0m/s2 so that the earthquake has died, the stiffness starts to stabilize in a value
called residual stiffness. From that, the residual periods will also be calculated being
it inverse proportional to the stiffness.
Overall, this final stabilization state can be treated as a pseudo-linear regime with a
degraded stiffness compared to the initial stiffness.

4 Validation of the Method
4.6
Validation of the mode shapes
This section aims to validate that the changes in the stiffness matrix cause changes
in the mode shapes. However, this time no time-history analysis will be run, just
two linear scenarios will be compared. One more time, there is no certainty about
the non.linear regime but there is about the linear elastic one and therefore, if two
scenarios with different stiffness are analyzed, then it can be extended to a change in
stiffness due to a nonlinear analysis.
The 2 scenarios with different stiffness that have been compared are the two-storey
reinforced concrete model described in section 4.2 with constant concrete strength of
35MPa and the same model that contains some changes in stiffness.
To introduce changes in the stiffness the material properties have to be changed.
Moreover, the changes must be done not uniformly so that the mode shapes will vary
compared to the original one. Thus, two models with uniform stiffness, even though
with different values, will result in equal mode shapes. Therefore, locally changing
the stiffness will help to understand better the variation of the mode shapes.
The first scenario, therefore, is formed by a constant strength value in all the sections
of the model, indeed 35MPa. However, the second scenario is formed by elements with
less strength, 18MPa, and others with the 35MPa. A sketch of the second scenario’s
elements’ strength is drawn in Figure 4.13.
Figure 4.13: Distribution of the concrete strength over the structure. Sketch of the
second scenario.
Furthermore, the steel properties have not been changed because it has been assumed
that the change in concrete would be sufficient to see the expected changes.
==PAGE:59==
4.6 Validation of the mode shapes
37
Since it is a static analysis, just running an eigenvalue analysis is enough to ob-
tain the results in Figure 4.14.
Those results show the expected variation due to the local differences in the stiffness.
Also, a clear trend can be seen for the variation of the higher modes, being bigger
than the lower ones which will be analyzed and observed also later in subsection 6.1.3.

CHAPTER 5
Model Description
The definition of the two used models is described in this section.
The first one is a simple two-storey and two base frame, very similar to the pre-
vious one (section 4.2), however, the sections and the sizes have been changed to
make it more realistic. From the side of the structural analysis, the sections and di-
mensions of the frame have been changed to create a model that could be the realistic
representation of an existing structure.
Furthermore, the use of this method has also been extended to a seven-storey and
nine base frame. This is an existing hotel model in California that has already been
investigated by other researchers and that now is used to track its modal parameters.
All the details are further explained in the next sections.
5.1
Two-storey and two-base frame
Figure 5.1: 2D and 2 storey frame with nodal lumped masses.
==PAGE:62==
40
5 Model Description
The MDOF system analyzed in the present study is a 2D frame composed of 2 storeys
and 2 bases. The mass is represented as 1tn lumped mass in each node in the three di-
rections (horizontal-translational, vertical-translational and rotational) and the bases
are fixed in the 3 directions as represented in Figure 4.6.
The columns side length is 0.4m and they all contain 8 rebars of 16mm of diame-
ter, whereas the beams have a section of 0.25m x 0.4m and contain 2 rebars of 18mm
in the positive moment side and 2 of 32mm in the negative moment side. The dis-
tribution of the rebars is described in Figure 5.2. The difference between the beams
and columns sections is due to the bending moment that the beams need to handle.
Following the basic structural theory, it is usual to make squared columns and rect-
angular beams.
Moreover, the dimensions of the frame correspond to the common values of span
length and storey height of normal buildings, being H1 and H2 equal to 3.5m and B
5.5m.
Coming back to the material properties, the characteristic strength and Young’s mod-
ulus of the concrete are 30MPa and 20GPa respectively. The steel, however, has a
strength of 550MPa and an elasticity module of 210GPa.The damping ratio is 0.02,
a commonly used value. All of the structure properties are collected and summarized
in Table 5.1 and 5.2.

5.1.1
Modelling in OpenSees.
To model this frame in OpenSees, several specifications need to be explained.
To define the elements the first election that has been done as previously explained
is to model the beams and columns without any mass and define the mass lumped
in the nodes. Furthermore, the mass will be added in the three directions (x, y, and
rotational direction).
To define the reinforced concrete section, Uniaxial Materials have been chosen because
the stress-strain or force-deformation relationship is provided by a single parameter,
the Elasticity Module.
At the same time, the section is defined as a fiber-based section which is a set of
uniaxial material sections located at specific areas. The advantage of using this kind
of section is that the results are more accurate because the analysis is discretized in
smaller areas. In addition, when the section is under bending moment, defining the
fibers give the opportunity of differentiating the compression and tension zones.
subsection A.2.2 describes how to model the fibers in OpenSees and an example is
also shown (see Figure A.3).
In this case, the degradation of the section will depend on the election of the compo-
nents of those fibers. Thus, ’steel02’ material has been chosen for the rebar definition
and ’concrete02’ for concrete the subsection A.2.1.
Furthermore, the equations and degrees of freedoms are listed using the ’plain num-
berer’ command which uses the defined node numbering order to list the nodes. The
DOF numbering, in this case, is done first numbering the horizontal-translational,
then the vertical-translational, and finally the rotational degrees of freedom. In con-
clusion, this numberer considers the node numbering order first and the degree of
freedom listing afterward.
An example of this is shown in Figure 5.3.
Finally, to be able to extract the full general system’s stiffness matrix, the system
needs to be defined as ’full general’. This command is used to construct a Full General
==PAGE:64==
42
5 Model Description
linear system equation object and even though it takes a lot of memory and time and
normally this is not used, it is very useful for the purpose of this method. Together
with the ’printA’ command, the generalized stiffness matrix for all time steps will be
obtained.
More detailed information about the OpenSees Modelling and Analysis commands
can be found in Appendix A.
Figure 5.3: Node and degree of freedom numbering when using the plain numberer
command.

5.2
Seven-storey and nine-base frame. A real hotel
model.
The simple two-storey frame model has been replaced by a seven-storey and nine base
frame that simulates a hotel (Holiday Inn) that was constructed in California, United
States of America [26]. It is a reinforced concrete building located at 8244 Orion Ave,
Van Nuys, California in the San Fernando Valley of Los Angeles County as shown in
Figure 5.4.
Figure 5.4: Holiday Inn Express hotel in San Fernando Valley, California, USA.
The hotel was built in 1966 and it was designed by Rissman and Rissman Associates
according to the 1964 Los Angeles City Building Code. It was designed to be com-
posed of reinforced concrete frames distributed in both directions to be able to resist
the lateral forces, i.e. the ground motion shake. The flat reinforced concrete slabs
also contribute to the lateral stiffness, which is supported by interior squared and
exterior rectangular columns.
Even though the hotel model uses American Measurement Units, the description
this time is done using the International System’s Units to be able to compare easier
the results and the models between them.
The hotel’s structure is 20m height in total distributed in 7 storeys where the ground
floor has a storey height of 4.1m that includes a lobby, a dining room, a tavern, a
banquet room, and other services’ rooms, and the rest of the floors’ height is 2.65m,
to fit the suites and a central corridor. The top floor is a bit higher, 2.75m.
Moreover, it is composed of 8 spans of 5.72m in the longitudinal direction and 3 spans
of 6.3m in the transverse direction. Figure 5.6 presents a plan view of a typical floor
==PAGE:66==
44
5 Model Description
and the side view of the traverse structural frame.
The slabs have different sizes depending on the height: 25.4cm thickness at the 2nd
floor, 21.6cm thickness from the 3rd to the 7th floor, and 20.3cm thickness at the
roof level. On top of the roof slab, a lightweight concrete topping was placed with
varying thicknesses from 8 cm to 20 cm.
The building is composed of four frames in the longitudinal direction as observed
in. However, the provided model [31] and used by a DTU student in 2019 [26] con-
siders only the south frame or frame A in the Figure 5.6. Overall, Figure 5.5 shows
the model’s sketch.
The model considers the mass of each storey as lumped masses on the nodes, being
different from floor to floor due to the differences in the slab thicknesses. Further-
more, the model is composed of three degrees of freedom in each node, summing up
a total of 189DOFs. However, the mass is defined as lumped for simplicity reasons
and only in the horizontal transitional direction because the earthquake is acting in
that direction as well. The nodes on the base of the building are fixed in all directions.
The main dimensions and material properties are summarized in Table 5.5, Table 5.3
and 5.4.
Furthermore, Los Angeles Building Department provided some structural and archi-
tectural details and drawings that have been collected in Appendix C and have been
used previously in other researches [25], [2], [3], and [32].

Furthermore, this model uses the ’RCM numberer’ command which provides the map-
ping between DOFs and equation numbers using RCM numbering. An RCM num-
berer uses the reverse Cuthill-McKee scheme to order the matrix equations, which
means that the listing is done for approximated nodes following the numbering of de-
grees of freedom as in the previous numbering type (horizontal-translational, vertical-
translational, and rotational).
==PAGE:70==
48
5 Model Description
Figure 5.7: Node and degree of freedom numbering when using the RCM numberer
command.
As an example, a two storey frame shown in Figure 5.7 is provided because it is
easier to compare the numberer with the previous model’s but, the hotel’s numberer
example has also been added and explained in Appendix C.
In this example, the numbering of the nodes shown in the image refers to the design
numbering (the numbering that was defined to model the structure). However, the
rcm renumbers the nodes and the degrees of freedom. In this case, it starts from the
top-right node (DOF 1, 2, and 3), continues with the two closest nodes (DOF 4, 5,
and 6 and DOF 7, 8, and 9), and finishes in the left bottom(DOF 10, 11, and 12).
Indeed, it follows the reverse Cuthill-McKee scheme, as mentioned previously.
The advantage of RCM numberer over plain numberer is that it renumbers the nodes,
being often possible to produce a matrix with a much smaller bandwidth reducing the
computational time, especially with complex systems with a large number of DOFs.
The model, in this case, has been adapted to be able to run the required analysis
for this specific method. The original model defined the system as ’UmfPack’ which
uses the UmfPack solver to construct a sparse system of equations [29]. However, as
previously stated, the system must be defined as ’fullGeneral’ because it is the only
command that makes it possible to extract the whole system’s stiffness matrix using
’printA’ command.
Furthermore, to applied the developed method, the definition of elastic materials
are needed so that the mass matrix and the initial elastic stiffness matrix are ob-
tained.
Therefore, the ’Nonlinear Beam/column’ of the original model have been
==PAGE:71==
5.2 Seven-storey and nine-base frame. A real hotel model.
49
used in the time history analysis, but replaced to run the pushover and the elastic
transient analyses. To do so ’Elastic Beam/column’ definition has been used.
A good way to verify the model is to run the eigenvalue analysis at all the steps
of the method and make sure that they match. Moreover, the validated provided
model’s fundamental elastic period is known and therefore it can be used to verify
the process as well. In this case the first mode’s fundamental elastic period according
to the method is 1.0176 whereas the original model’s is 1.02.

CHAPTER 6
Results
The previously described models’ have been analyzed and this section contains the de-
scription and explanation of the obtained results. Furthermore, the modal parameters
that have been analyzed over time for the different modes are the period evolution,
the residual periods, the mode shapes, and the residual mode shapes, and because of
that, each model’s results are divided into those subsections.
6.1
A simple two-storey and two-base model
This model’s properties and dimensions are shown in section 5.1. Before analyzing
the dynamic properties, and because the main focus of this study is the analysis of
the structures in the nonlinear regime, the study needs to be calibrated, so that it
is ensured to be acting in the nonlinear regime. To do so, the load factor needs to
be applied so that the earthquake load is scaled up to make it overcome the linear
behavior. Plotting the hysteric loop of a section in the base will make it possible to
observe these nonlinearities.
Furthermore, the response of the model also needs to be observed to assure the be-
havior of the system and how degradation affects it. Because of that, the response,
the ground motion, and the stiffness degradation are plotted together to see over time
the relation between them. All of these representations are shown in Figure 6.1
The hysteretic loop of the system that is plotted in Figure 6.1 which seems to follow
the material laws.
The plot also shows the displacement of the top-right node in the transitional hori-
zontal direction, and the stiffness degradation is measured also for the transitional
degree of freedom in the top element section. Finally, the ground motion, in this case,
El-Centro, is also plotted to be able to observe the relation between degradation, re-
sponse, and ground motion every time instant.
==PAGE:74==
52
6 Results

6.1.1
Period Evolution
The results shown in this section correspond to El-Centro strong ground motion and
the fundamental elastic period of the structure is 0.147sec. The period evolution of
this system is represented in Figure 6.2 for the 12 modes of the system. As previously
stated, the system has 12 degrees-of-freedom and therefore it will have 12 periods.
Even though in reality the higher modes have a very small participation factor and
therefore it does not make sense to observe them, it has been decided to do so. Since
this structure has a low number of degrees of freedom compared to the full hotel
model (section 5.2) and it is the first time that this method is applied, it has been
considered that it would be interesting to widen the research and look into those
higher modes as well.
Those periods are calculated from the instantaneous stiffness matrices obtained using
OpenSees. Running several eigenvalue analyses, one for each time step, the different
frequencies are obtained and consequently the periods.

Looking at the just mentioned plot, the linearity of the first 6.5 seconds, approxi-
mately, can easily be identified. As expected and explained in the verification process,
the stiffness does not degrade until that specific time instant, and the whole system
will remain elastic, meaning that also, the period will be constant.
However, after that initial state, the period starts to elongate due to a decay in the
stiffness. Moreover, there are fluctuations in the evolution of the period overtime
being the high peaks representative of smaller stiffness time instants. It is observed
==PAGE:76==
54
6 Results
that the lower modes have the most significant stiffness reduction meaning that they
are more sensitive to stiffness changes.
Furthermore, the results are verified because if a simple linear analysis is done and
the initial elastic period is obtained, it coincides with the first time instant’s period
as previously explained in the verification process.
Overall, from that figure, it is be concluded that the lower modes are more sensi-
tive to changes in stiffness over time looking at the fluctuations on the evolution,
even though this trend is not uniform in all the modes as they are higher.
Additionally, the plot is representing the period elongation of each of the modes
as the initial period is the lowest and it increases over time. However, for better ob-
servation and study of the results, each mode’s elastic period has been compared with
the instantaneous period so that the period elongation can be measured every time
instant. This is what Figure 6.3 represents, the ratio between the first time instants’
period of each mode, or the elastic period of each mode, and the instantaneous period
or the period at every 0.005sec.

As a conclusion on period elongation, it is observed that the lower modes are the most
affected ones by stiffness degradation and because of that suffer the highest period
==PAGE:77==
6.1 A simple two-storey and two-base model
55
elongation, especially the first and second modes. It is also clear from the figures that
the highest modes, 11 and 12, are the ones less sensitive to period elongation, and
because of that, they remain more constant in time. However, no clear conclusion
can be obtained for the rest of the modes because they don’t follow any obvious trend.
There are several reasons for not being able to obtain clear conclusions about the
intermediate modes, but one of them is the mixing of the modes due to the proximity
of the elastic periods of each mode, i.e. modes 11 and 12. This will be discussed later
on the mode shapes analysis section (subsection 6.1.3).
However, this phenomenon does not explain the longer elongation of some high modes
compared to low ones when their periods are different, i.e. mode 6 which is bellow
the elongation of 10th mode. This can be explained by the low participation that
those modes have. It is important to mention that the fundamental period of the first
mode is already very small and therefore, it is not possible to obtain big conclusions
about very high modes because their elastic period are almost insignificant.
Furthermore, this is just a representation of a specific case and earthquake but the
analysis was run with several earthquakes and the same trend was observed being
those conclusions strong. However, this study contains results for a second and more
complex model to be able to corroborate the method, the results, and the conclusions
(section 6.2).
6.1.2
Residual Periods
Residual periods by definition are the remaining vibration periods of the structure af-
ter the earthquake load has been applied. From Figure 6.2 it could be concluded that
the residual period is the last point of the figure for each mode, however, a sudden
stop is not considered to be representative of the reality, being needed a stabilization
of the system after the earthquake to have realistic results.
Because of that, in this section 25s of 0m/s2 have been added to the ground motion
to extend the analysis and forcing the system to stabilize after the shock.
Looking at these results, the period evolution seems to be as expected (Figure 6.4),
the first 6.5 seconds of the elastic regime or constant stiffness and period, the next
33.5 seconds of nonlinear behavior where the degradation of the stiffness is reflected
as period elongation and fluctuations on the period evolution, and the last 25 seconds
where the system stabilizes in a higher period than the initial one representing the
period elongation. It is also visible, how the period curve smoothens and reaches the
stable part at the end matching the time steps where the earthquake stops and 0
acceleration is introduced.
As expected, when the system degrades, the period of the structure elongates and
when the earthquake stops, a new scenario is presented, a stabilized system with a
==PAGE:78==
56
6 Results
smaller stiffness. It is observed that when this stabilized system is reached, the pe-
riod is elongated which reflects that the stiffness is smaller due to the damages and a
pseudo-linear state is shown.
Figure 6.4: Two-storey and two-base RC model’s period evolution over time when
subjected to El-Centro strong ground motion. Observation of the resid-
ual periods by introducing 25sec of 0 acceleration. (a) Represented on
a logarithmic scale. (b) Represented on a normal scale.
Figure 6.5 demonstrates and confirms that the trend that was observed for the pe-
riod evolution during the excitation of the earthquake is also reflected in the residual
==PAGE:79==
6.1 A simple two-storey and two-base model
57
periods. It is observed that the lower modes are more sensitive to the stiffness degra-
dation and because of that not only the elongation is bigger during the earthquake
but also, the residual period elongation is significantly more severe.
Moreover, the higher periods also demonstrate the trend of the previous section. They
can be concluded to be less affected and therefore, the residual period elongation is
also smaller on those modes.
The intermediate modes do not follow any trend either for the period elongation and
because of that, any robust conclusion can be obtained. However, the mixing mode
phenomena previously explained could also explain the reason why those intermediate
modes do not follow any pattern.
Figure 6.5: Period elongation over time represented as a ratio between the instanta-
neous period of the structure with the initial elastic period considering
also the added 25seconds of zero acceleration..
6.1.3
Mode Shapes Evolution
The mode shapes or vibration modes by definition describe the configurations or the
patterns in which a structure will displace at a specific frequency and therefore they
are directly correlated with the period. The eigenvalue problem provides the eigen-
value or frequency and the eigenvector or the mode shape vector which is dependent
on the stiffness and the mass. Since the mass is constant over time, any variation on
the mode shape is directly related to changes on the stiffness matrix, and therefore, it
is expected a deviation because of the stiffness degradation. Figure 6.6 and 6.7 show
the evolution of the mode shapes over time when subjected to earthquake excitation.
==PAGE:80==

6 Results

The plots show two different scenarios. The blue line represents the elastic mode
shape, in other words, the mode shape obtained at the first time instant. On the
contrary, the red lines represent the mode shapes evolution over time, which is the
==PAGE:82==
60
6 Results
same as saying the mode shape obtained from the eigenvalue analysis for the different
stiffness matrices at every 0.005 seconds.
Looking at the results, it is observable that the lower modes are not so much af-
fected by the changes in the stiffness matrix than the higher modes. On the contrary
to what it has been concluded from the period evolution, the higher modes seem to
be significantly more sensitive than the lower ones to the changes in mode shapes due
to stiffness variation.
Furthermore, the higher modes such as modes 9 and 10 show some perturbations
in the mode shapes.
The elastic mode shape doesn’t match the nonlinear mode
shape at specific times and this is related to the fact that the modes are mixing.
The phenomena explained when the period evolution results were shown, is visually
more reflected in the mode shapes. When the periods are very close to each other
the modes mix and because of that, it can be seen that the shapes sometimes match
better the elastic shapes of other modes.
To make sure that this hypothesis is correct, the MAC values of the elastic and
nonlinear mode shapes have been compared in the next section.
6.1.3.1
MAC values
The MAC values are used to compare the similarities between two plots providing as
an output a number between 0 and 1, where 1 means that they are completely equal
and 0 being totally different.

Figure 6.8 provides a robust conclusion about the first six modes. As expected from
the mode shapes figure (Figure 6.6) all of their shapes match their corresponding elas-
tic mode’s mode shape. This is confirmed because all of the MAC values are above
0.95 except for a few peaks that go bellow that up to 0.75. Those peaks represent
specific time instants in which the nonlinear mode shapes vary more than at other
time instants, but they are not determining because they are just very few values at
very specific time steps.
On the other hand, Figure 6.9 represents a very different scenario. Looking at the
mode shapes (Figure 6.7) it was expected to be seen some mixing of the modes be-
cause some mode shapes do not match their corresponding linear mode shape but
other mode’s linear mode shape. This is confirmed in Figure 6.9 where the MAC
values are extremely low especially for the case of modes 9 and 10. However, the
hypothesis of the mixing modes needs to be corroborated to make conclusions and
therefore, the MAC value criterion has been used to sort them to their corresponding
mode.
This criterion consists on using the MAC values to compare the instantaneous mode
shapes of the 12 modes with the 12 elastic mode shapes. By making this comparison
each nonlinear mode shape will be sorted to their corresponding mode, making sure
that the instantaneous and the linear mode shapes match. The results are shown in
Figure 6.8 and 6.9.

These figures corroborate the hypothesis of the mixing modes. Figure 6.11 shows an
increase in the MAC values of the higher modes, i.e. the 9th and 10th mode have
increased over 0.5 and this is because the mode shapes have been sorted to their
corresponding mode.
Moreover, the values of the lower modes have not changed, as it was expected, this
didn’t suffer mixing of the modes in this specific model.
Looking more in deep to the sorted MAC values evolution, there is a clear difference
between the variation of the mode shapes for lower and higher modes. Figure 6.8
shows that the lower modes are less sensitive to the changes in the stiffness matrix,
remaining very close to the linear elastic mode shape. Furthermore, among those
first six modes, it is very clear to observe that the sixth mode vary considerably more
than the first one, meaning that the lower the mode the smaller the variation of the
mode shape over time.
However, Figure 6.9 shows that the higher modes vary significantly more than the
lower ones. Overall, it is concluded also that the higher the mode the more sensitive
the mode shape is to changes in stiffness, i.e. the 12th and 11th modes are clearly
the most affected ones looking at the sorted results.
To conclude with the analysis of the mode shapes, the sorted mode shapes’ MAC
values have been represented in a histogram to make final conclusions about the
==PAGE:86==
64
6 Results
different modes’ behaviour (see Figure 6.12 and 6.13)
Figure 6.12: Two-storey and two-base RC frame’s histogram of the accumulated
MAC values of the sorted mode shapes of modes 1 to 6.
Figure 6.13: Two-storey and two-base RC frame’s histogram of the accumulated
MAC values of the sorted mode shapes of modes 7 to 12.
==PAGE:87==
6.1 A simple two-storey and two-base model
65
Those histograms show the accumulated MAC values at every time instant and they
just confirmed the previously stated conclusions and observations. The lower modes
are less sensitive to changes in mode shapes over time whereas the higher modes
change a lot with time.
Furthermore, while the lower modes are all above 0.97, the higher modes present
two trends of accumulation. This is because the stiffness does not degrade until it
reaches a specific time instant, and before that, the linear mode shape is the same
as the nonlinear one so the MAC value is 1. After degrading, however, the deviation
from the elastic mode shape is significantly bigger than the lower modes, being this
represented with accumulated MAC values of around 0.5 to 0.7.
Therefore, after sorting the data it can be concluded that the higher the mode, the
bigger is the variation of its mode shape over time.
6.1.4
Residual Mode Shapes
Finally, the residual mode shapes have been investigated in this section in the same
way the residual periods have been analyzed. Using the ground motion as described
in subsection 6.1.2, indeed with the added 25 seconds of 0 acceleration, the mode
shapes corresponding to the stabilized state has been plotted and analyzed.
Figure 6.14 shows two mode shapes. The blue line corresponds to the initial or elastic
mode shape for the first 6 modes, whereas the red line shows the residual mode shape,
or in other words, the last point’s mode shape after the system has stabilized when
the earthquake load has finalized.
Looking at the results, it seems that the variation of the mode shape is bigger dur-
ing the excitation than after, and because of that, the residual mode shape doesn’t
change that much compared to the elastic mode shape.
The reason for this conclusion, which seems to be completely different from the con-
clusions of the residual periods, is that only the global mode shapes are visible in
these figures. As it was explained in section 4.6, if two identical systems with uni-
form stiffness but different values are compared, the mode shapes are going to be
identical, just local stiffness changes will make them vary. Therefore, what it seems
to happen is that over time the variation of the stiffness is changing but when it
reaches the stabilized state where the acceleration is 0, the stiffness is uniform even
though it has degraded and not too many variations are visible from the elastic mode
shape.
Therefore, if the analysis would have been done locally, it is believed that more
changes would be visible.

6.2
Realistic seven-storey and nine-base model
After focusing on the results of the two-storey reinforced concrete frame, it has been
decided to extend the research to a more complex model that contains more degrees
of freedom and study the use of the explained method on that model. The chosen
model was decided to be a verified one, explained in section 5.2, to assure that the
model provides realistic results.
Hence, the present section shows the results for the period evolution, and mode
shapes evolution over time, as well as the residual periods and mode shapes for the
hotel model described in section 5.2.
Furthermore, to analyze the effect of the load factor or in other words, the strength
of an earthquake, on the results of the period elongation, the same earthquake but
with several load factors have been analyzed for this specific model. El- Centro strong
ground motion (Figure 4.1) has been used for that and the three load factors that
have been applied are 0.7, 1.5, and 2.0.
6.2.1
Period Evolution
This hotel model has 189 DOFs, however, in real life, it is unusual to analyze higher
modes because they have lower participation and their effect is so small that it can be
neglected. Therefore, the study of the different modes’ behavior is limited to 6 modes.
Figure 6.15 demonstrate the period evolution overtime after having extracted the
stiffness matrix of the full general system every time instant and calculated the pe-
riod from the eigenvalue analysis. In this case, the load factor that has been analyzed
is 0.7. The trend of the graph is very similar to the previously analyzed model (sec-
tion 4.2) where it is observable that the first 9 seconds of the earthquake the system
remains linear, no stiffness degradation is shown and therefore there are no fluctua-
tions in the period evolution remaining completely constant.
After that, the degradation starts to show up in terms of fluctuating period and
period elongation. It is very clear in Figure 6.16 that the lower modes suffer more
elongation than the higher ones as it was expected from previous results.
Additionally, one more time it is not easy to conclude anything for the higher modes,
even though there seems to be a trend to elongate less than the lower ones, this is
not always respected, i.e. mode 3 elongates less than modes 4 and 5.

6.2.2
Residual Periods
The method used for the calculation of the residual period is identical to the two-
storey frame’s model. The only difference is that since in this case, the system is
significantly more complex, the stabilization process after the earthquake load has
died is longer and therefore, adding 25 seconds of 0m/s2 acceleration to the ground
motion is not sufficient. Because of that 60 seconds have been added in this specific
model showing the linear behavior of the damaged system after the earthquake. Fig-
ure 6.17 shows results of the residual periods and period evolution corresponding to
a load factor of 0.7 as well.
As observed in Figure 6.17, the residual period is different from the initial elastic
period due to period elongation when the structure has been damaged during the
excitation of the ground motion. The damages are shown as stiffness degradation
and those, at the same time are reflected in period elongations. The stability shown
in the period, just reflect an stable state after the shock with a smaller stiffness and
longer period.

6 Results

strong ground motion. Observation of the residual periods by intro-
ducing 60sec of 0 acceleration. Load factor of 0.7. (a) Represented in
logarithmic scale. (b) Represented in a normal scale.
