is also visualized here. One more time clear conclusions can only be obtained for the
first two modes where the elongation is considerably more severe as represented in
Figure 6.18.
Since those results only demonstrate the trend for an specific earthquake, the re-
search of the residual period has been extended to see the effect of the different load
factors on the results. The strength of an earthquake is a factor that should be re-
flected on the results, specially on the elongation and because of that, it needs to be
analyzed. The period evolution, the elongation and the residual periods are shown in
the figures bellow for their corresponding load factors.

visible in Table 6.1.

It is expected that a higher load will affect the period elongation since the degrada-
tion is bigger. Consequently, the residual damage is also going to increase when the
damage increases and this is what it is observed in Table 6.1. Not only the increase
of the residual period elongation is happening in all modes, but also, the lower the
mode, the bigger it is the increase. All of this, in conclusion, corroborates what has
previously been explained that the lower the mode, the more sensitive it is to period
elongation, in this case, when increasing the earthquake load.
Secondly, when looking at the non-logarithmic period evolution plot, some 0 values
are shown and some pronounced positive peaks as well. The high peaks are related
to very small stiffness that is close to zero and the lower ones are due to a complex
eigenvalue solution at that specific time instants.
The complex eigenvalue solution comes from some negative components of the stiff-
ness. Those values are believed to be caused due to a numerical or modeling issue.
Even though the model is verified because the response that was measured with ac-
celerograms in different locations and the simulated one in OpenSees were very close
to each other, the method that has been applied to the model this time provides
additional outputs that have not been verified, as it is the stiffness matrix every time
instant. Because of that, some time instants do not provide trustful results but those
can be neglected because each of them just represents 0.005 seconds out of 39 sec-
onds of earthquake duration. Furthermore, they are so few points that obtaining any
robust conclusion out of them is pointless.
The last observation, that is more mathematical, is that in the period elongation
plot and in the non-logarithmic period evolution representation over time, these zero
value points are shown whereas in the logarithmic scale plot there are not. This is
==PAGE:98==
76
6 Results
because the logarithm of a zero value does not have an answer and Matlab does plots
it as a gap, whereas in the other cases, the imaginary numbers are treated just taking
the real number part.
6.2.3
Mode Shapes Evolution
The mode shapes evolution over time has been plotted in this section for the three
load factors to analyze the results from the method and make robust conclusions.
Moreover, following the conclusions from the previous section, it has not been consid-
ered the time steps where the period goes to zero. As stated, the time instants are
very small and therefore it is considered that their contribution is also insignificant.
Figure 6.23 corroborates the results and conclusions that have been done for a two-
storey more simple frame.
The lower the mode, the smaller is the effect of the
degradation on the mode shape. Those changes are mostly observed comparing load
factor 0.7 and 2 where, specially mode 6 varies more the higher the load factor is.
Besides, as observed in modes 5 and 6 it seems to be some deviations which can be
the consequence of mixing modes. Therefore, it has been decided to sort them using
the MAC values to make sure that these conclusions are right.
First of all, the MAC values have been obtained comparing those first six modes’
shapes every time instant with all of the mode’s first initial mode shape. This has
been done for the highest load factor, 2. Figure 6.26 shows all the instantaneous MAC
values for the six modes where, as expected, the lower the mode the more similar is
to the elastic mode shape. However, the MAC values for mode 6 are extremely small
and that puts in doubt the results (see Figure 6.29).
Because of that, an step forward has been done and instead of sorting them just
looking at the first six modes, they have been sorted looking at the first 8.
The
reason for doing so is that the mixing of the 6th mode could be with the 7th.
Figure 6.28 and 6.29 demonstrate the just mentioned theory were it can be observed
how the sixth mode is now closer to the fifth and above 50% of similarity with the
elastic mode shape.
Furthermore, it is observed that the higher the load factor the more severe are the
effect of the nonlinearities, and the more the mode shapes change. It has also been
analyzed the earthquake with the same 3 load factors. Thus the results for 0.7, 1.5,
and 2 are summarized in the figures below.

6.2.4
Residual Mode shapes
The residual mode shapes have been obtained in the same way as the previous model.
This time, the earthquake has 60seconds of 0m/s2 added acceleration to be able to
analyze the stabilized system after the earthquake shake has stopped.
The results shown in Figure 6.30, 6.31 and 6.32 corroborate the results previously
obtained for the two-storey simple frame. As stated in subsection 6.1.4 the varia-
tion of the mode shapes over the excitation is more visible than after that because
the damages are changing so the reduction of the stiffness is changing but the mode
shapes that are being plotted are global mode shapes. It is therefore believed that, if
the local damages where reflected in the mode shapes (same as done in section 4.6),
the mode shapes would vary more.
Moreover, the differences in the load factors are not so easily recognized in this case
because the variation is very small. THerefore, the MAC values have been obtained to
compare the residual mode shapes with the initial elastic ones and also, compare the
effect of the strength of the earthquake on them. Table 6.2 show the just mentioned
MAC values.

CHAPTER 7
Conclusions
The main focus of this work is the investigation of the evolution of the modal parame-
ters of MDOF systems when they are subjected to strong ground motion accelerations.
Based on an existing robust and proven method previously applied to SDOFs where,
by the use of the finite element model software OpenSees, it is possible to extract the
full general system’s stiffness matrix every time instant, the period evolution and the
mode shapes evolution over time has been extracted. Furthermore, the residual peri-
ods and mode shapes have also been determined to be able to quantify the damage
after the shock of the ground motion.
The analyses that have been run cover two main models that have been indepen-
dently analyzed. Several time history analyses with different earthquakes and load
factors have been run. However, the extent of this research has finalized with the anal-
ysis of a single earthquake but several load factors, to visualize and obtain results
for the period elongation and mode shapes variation overtime when the earthquake
is stronger or weaker. All of the results obtained during the research seemed to have
the same trend and therefore, the conclusions summarized in this section are robust
and trustful.
Moreover, the hotel model is a verified model that has been used to be able to assure
that the method works also with a real structure. The measured responses that the
accelerograms recorded were compared with the OpenSees model’s output responses
which concluded being very similar.
Therefore, the analysis of the dynamic properties over time has lead to the following
conclusions:
• When the stiffness degrades, the period starts to elongate. There is a clear ob-
servation that at the beginning of the earthquake, the stiffness remains constant
and so is the period over time. However, the stiffness starts to drop when the
system enters the nonlinear regime and the period starts to show the elongation
and instability because some fluctuations are observed.
• While the stiffness matrix varies, the different modes’ period starts to elongate
in different ways. Overall, the first and the second mode, which are the ones
that have bigger participation and the earthquake affects them the most, seem
==PAGE:110==
88
7 Conclusions
to be the most sensitive to period elongation. Higher modes, on the contrary,
seem to be less affected by period elongation, but this is not always fulfilled. It
can therefore not be concluded that the higher the mode the lower the elonga-
tion because some factors are affecting those results, i.e mixing of the modes
when the fundamental elastic periods of different modes are closer to each other.
Moreover, the higher modes can often be neglected due to their low participation
and their small fundamental period.
• The mode shapes also show a nonlinear behavior. Especially when the fluctu-
ations in the stiffness matrix and the period evolution are visible, the mode
shapes vary considerably due to the damages changing locally. Furthermore,
the linear behavior at the beginning of the earthquake shock is also reflected as
constant mode shapes.
• The different modes’ shapes show the opposite trend than what it was expected
after analyzing the period elongations.
The higher modes seem to be more
sensitive to changes in the mode shapes and the lower ones almost do not
change over time. Furthermore, it has been analyzed that there is a trend to
mix modes when the fundamental period of the different modes are close to
each other, and a MAC value comparison criterion has been developed to sort
them and be able to obtain clear conclusions.
• Even though the analysis with different earthquakes has not been done, the
effect of applying different load factors to the same earthquake has been proven
using the hotel model. This has resulted in an expected conclusion, being the
effect of a higher load, or bigger earthquake, higher in the period elongation
than the lower load factors. Moreover, looking at the different modes, there
is a clear tendency to observe that the elongation of the lower modes increases
proportionally more than the higher ones, which concluded that the lower modes
are more sensitive to the earthquake load intensity as well.
• Looking at the effect of the load factors on the mode shapes, it is concluded
that a bigger seismic force leads to a bigger nonlinear response and therefore
a bigger change in the mode shapes. Furthermore, it seems that the effect on
higher modes is more severe than in lower ones as it increases being the trend
of more affected higher modes also corroborated.
• The residual periods reflect that after the structure being damaged, there is an
elongation in all the modes’ periods as it was expected. Furthermore, the lower
modes show the biggest sensitivity to those changes over the period evolution
and that tendency remained after the earthquake load was released and the new
pseudo-linear state is achieved.
• Finally, the residual mode shapes show a completely different scenario during
the earthquake and after. They seemed to be more affected while the load was
acting and this is because the changes in the stiffness matrices were varying in
==PAGE:111==
7 Conclusions
89
a non-uniform way. However, a pseudo-linear state is reached afterward, with
the new stiffness matrix after the degradation and since the mode shapes are
plotted globally, it is not visible that big change in the mode shapes. Therefore,
the damages cannot be quantified looking at the residual mode shapes, and
therefore, more local research and changes should be analyzed to obtain better
conclusions.
==PAGE:112==
90
==PAGE:113==
CHAPTER 8
Further Work
This chapter discusses the further possible studies and researches that can be done
under structural deterioration topic when subjected to seismic excitations using this
method. The method makes it possible to obtain results for the modal parameters
every time instant being able to have accurate results on the effects of earthquakes on
structural damages. However, in this study, the possibility of extending the method
to MDOFs has been analyzed and verify, but still there is a wide range of cases that
haven’t been analyzed. Because of that several possible researches are proposed in
this section to widen the knowledge on this topic:
• Populate the results of the MDOF systems by applying the method to an struc-
ture subjected to several earthquakes to analyze the effects of the different
earthquake loads on the response and on the degradation. By doing so, not
only the method will be verified, but also, the effect of the frequency content on
the different mode’s will be analyzed and more robust results will be acquired.
• Investigate different types of structures, starting from asymmetric frames and
continuing with 3D models. Even though the computational time can be very
big, it would be interesting to model a full 3D structure and apply this method.
• Observe how seismic sequences affect the method and how the responses and
the different dynamic properties of the system vary with those kind of ground
motions.
• Analyze the evolution damping of the MDOF systems. This is another modal
parameter that should be involved in this study because it also helps to quantify
the damages and understand how the stiffness and strength degradation affects
it.
• Analyze and observe several types of models by using other types of materials
for the fiber-based sections or just by using other material models that also
consider the degradation like Bouc Wen material or Ibarra-Medina-Krawinkler
for MDOF systems. It would be interesting to observe the application of other
uniaxial materials like the ’steel01’ and ’concrete01’ to be able to observe how
the different material laws could affect or not the method.
==PAGE:114==
92
==PAGE:115==
APPENDIX A
OpenSees Used
Functions
In this Annex, the used OpenSees commands are described. Indeed modeling and
analysis tools.
A.1
Elements
There are several types of elements between OpenSees modelling commands but since
in this research just frames are going to be analyzed only the 2D modelling elements
are going to be discussed in this section.
To model a frame just the definition of two points or nodes are needed with their
corresponding coordinates. A frame element is modeled as a straight line connecting
two points. There are several commands to define those elements which are described
in this section.
A.1.1
Elastic Beam-Column Element
This type of element is used to represent beams and columns with linear elastic
behavior.
The input properties are: cross sectional area of the element, Young’s
modulus, shear modulus, torsional inertia, and the moment of inertia about the local
axes of the element.
A.1.2
Force Beam-Column Element
However, when the nonlinear behaviour of the model is needed to be tracked the
element must be defined in the nonlinear regime or in other words, with some plasticity.
Because of that, the Force-Based Beam-Column Element is used to define the elements
of the frame which is based on the iterative force-based formulation. A variety of
numerical integration options can be used in the element state determination and
encompass both distributed plasticity and plastic hinge integration [29]. The input
parameters in this case are the element mass density number of integration and the
locations and weight of the integration points.
==PAGE:116==
94
A OpenSees Used Functions
A.1.3
Nonlinear Beam-Column Element
This type of element provides the same definition as the previous one (subsection A.1.2)
despite some apparent permutations of the input arguments which just differ in the
numerical integration type.
A.2
Material Models
A.2.1
Uniaxial Material
This command is used to define an elastic uniaxial material that provides a stress-
strain or force-deformation relationship by defining a single parameter, the Elasticity
Modulus. Some examples are described in the present section for the different mate-
rials.
A.2.1.1
Steel02
The Steel02 uniaxial material constructs a Giuffre-Menegotto-Pinto steel material
object that has an isotropic strain hardening [29]. It provides control between elastic
to plastic transition (Figure A.1). As in steel 01, the isotropic hardening is an optional
parameter.
Figure A.1: Definition of Steel02 material [29].
A.2.1.2
Concrete 02
On the other hand, The concrete02 uniaxial material is used to provide tensile
strength and linear tension softening. It is visible the stiffness degradation in a linear
way for reloading/unloading.
==PAGE:117==
A.2 Material Models
95
Figure A.2: Definition of Concrete 02 material [29].
A.2.2
Fiber-Based model
Figure A.3: Concrete fibers in RC section. Fibers in the perpendicular direction to
the bending moment axis.
A fiber-based section is a set of uniaxial material sections that are located at specific
areas and locations. Each of those smaller sections are called fibers and give more
accurate results because the analysis is run in a smaller and detailed way. In other
words, the section is discretized by defining several fibers composed of uniaxial mate-
rial.
In OpenSees the command that is used is section Fiber, which allows users to define
==PAGE:118==
96
A OpenSees Used Functions
the material properties of each fiber which have regular shapes (e.g. quadrilateral,
circular, and triangular regions) with the patch command. Also, to be able to model
the concrete sections, the reinforcement bars are defined with he layer command.
In this case, the degradation of the section will depend on the election of the compo-
nents of those fibers, i.e. the two uniaxial materials defined in section A.2 provides
stiffness and strength degradation. In this case, therefore, the degradation change is
done indirectly, by changing the material properties.
An example of a fiber based model is shown in Figure A.3
A.3
System Definition
This command is used to solve and store system equations during the analysis. There
are several types of commands that can be used for this purpose, however, the method
that is used in this study requires the extraction of the full system’s generalized stiff-
ness matrix, and therefore, ’system FullGeneral’ will be used.
This command is used to construct a Full General linear system of equation object
[29]. Even though it is very useful for the method is being followed for this thesis,
it is a big disadvantage the amount of memory that it requires to run, making the
computational time increase when the matrix dimensions increase.
A.4
Extract General Full Stiffness Matrix of the system
As already mentioned, the generalized stiffness matrix of the entire system is going to
be needed and to do so ’Print A’ command is used, always taking into consideration
that previously the system must be defined as full general.
This command is used to print the current global system matrix if the static inte-
grator is used, but if a transient integrator is used instead, the printed matrix will be
some combination of mass and stiffness matrices.
==PAGE:119==

B.3 Matlab Script for analytical initial stiffness matrix calculation. 2 storey RC frame. 101
B.3
Matlab Script for analytical initial stiffness matrix
calculation. 2 storey RC frame.
1 Ec=20*10^9;
2 Es=210*10^9;
3 dtop=0.012;
4 dbottom=0.012;
5 Atop=4*pi*dtop^2/4;
6 Abottom=4*pi*dbottom^2/4;
7 h=0.25;
8 b=0.25;
9 Ac=b*h;
10 n=Es/Ec;
11 cover=0.050;
12 hbottom=h-cover-dbottom/2;
13 htop=cover+dtop/2;
14 A=Ac+(n-1)*Atop+(n-1)*Abottom;
15 gc=h/2;
16 Iz=(b*h^(3)/12)+b*h*(gc-h/2)^2+(n-1)*Abottom*(hbottom-gc)^2+(n-1)*Atop*(htop-gc
)^2;
17 L=8;
18 E0=Ec;

==PAGE:127==
APPENDIX C
Hotel model
In this Annex, all the data provided for the hotel model has been collected and ex-
plained.
First of all, the DOF numbering has been specified where the RCM numbering is
used. As explained in section 5.2 the numbering of the degrees of freedom and there-
fore of the equations is done by approximated nodes. Starting from the top right node
(DOFs 1, 2 and 3) then counting the two nodes closer to it in the top-left (DOFs 4,
5 and 6) to bottom-right (DOFs 7, 8 and 9) direction and at the end finishing in the
bottom-left node (DOFs 187, 188 and 189).
Figure C.1: 2D and 2 storey frame with nodal lumped masses.
Furthermore, the elements have been numbered to define them as it follows:
C.1
Structural and Architectural Details Drawings
Finally, all the architectural and structural details provided [26] have been also in-
cluded in the present appendix.

1 clear all; close all; clc;

20 # Define static load
21

23
24 pattern Plain 1 Linear {
25
load $tLf2 1000. 0. 0.;
26 }
27

29 # Define recorder(s)
30 set dir output_static2storeys
31 recorder Node
-file $dir/$record/DFree_static.out -time -node $tRf2
-dof 1
2 3 disp;
32 recorder Element -file $dir/$record/Force_static.out -time -ele
$colRf1
section 1 force;
33 recorder Element -file $dir/$record/Def_static.out
-time -ele
$colRf1
section 1 deformation;
34

Plain
38
39

Plain
41

+1.0E-12 100

Newton
47

49 set Dmax
[expr 0.05*$H2]; # maximum displacement of pushover
==PAGE:188==
166
G OpenSees Scripts of the two-storey RC frame
50 set Dincr [expr 0.00001*$H2];
# increment of pushover
51 integrator DisplacementControl $tLf2 1 $Dincr;
52 analysis Static;
53
54
55 # ---- Analyze model
56 set Nsteps [expr int($Dmax/$Dincr)];
# number of pushover analysis steps
57 analyze $Nsteps;
58
59 # ---- prints generalized stiffness matrix
60 printA -file $dir/$record/GenStiffMat/K_gen_static.out;
61
62
63
64 printA;
65 wipe;

G.4 Elastic transient analysis. Step 2
167
G.4
Elastic transient analysis. Step 2
1 set materialModel elastic;
# (choice: elastic/nonlinear)
2 source inputParameters2Storeys.tcl
3 source modelDefinition2Storeys.tcl
4 source loadDefinition.tcl
5
6
7 # Define recorder(s)
8 set dir output_trans2storeys
9 recorder Node
-file $dir/$record/DFree_trans.out -time -node $tRf2
-dof 1
2 3 disp;
10 recorder Element -file $dir/$record/Force_trans.out -time -ele
$colRf1 section
1 force;
11 recorder Element -file $dir/$record/Def_trans.out
-time -ele
$colRf1 section
1 deformation;
12
13 # Natural frequency of system
14 # ---------------------------
15 set lambda [eigen -fullGenLapack 1];
# eigenvalue mode 1
16 set omega [expr $lambda**0.5];
# circular natural frequency of mode 1
17 set filename "$dir/$record/omega.out"
18 set fileId [open $filename "w"];
19 puts $fileId $omega;
20 close $fileId;
21
22 source createTransientAnalysis.tcl
23
24
25 # Analyze model
26
27 # -------------
28 set t 0.0;
29 set ok 0.0;
30 set maxT [expr (1+$nPts)*$dT];
31 while {$ok == 0 && $t<$maxT} {
32
set ok [analyze 1 $dT]
33
set t [getTime]
34
# names and prints generalised stiffness matrix to file:
35
set Kfile [join [concat "K_gen_trans" "$t" ".out"] "" ];
36
printA -file $dir/$record/GenStiffMat/$Kfile;
37 }
38
39 wipe;
.

G.5
Nonlinear analysis. Step 3.
1 set materialModel nonlinear;
# (choice: elastic/nonlinear)

5
6

2 3 disp;

$colRf1
section 1 force;
11 recorder Element -file $dir/$record/Def_nonlin.out
-time -ele
$colRf1
section 1 deformation;
12
13 source damping.tcl
14 source createTransientAnalysis.tcl
15 puts $omega
16
17
18 # ---- Analyze
19
20 set t 0.0;
21 set ok 0.0;
22 set maxT [expr (1+$nPts)*$dT];
23 while {$ok == 0 && $t<$maxT} {
24
set ok [analyze 1 $dT]
25
set t [getTime]
26
# names and prints generalised stiffness matrix to file:
27
